Source: Software Development with AI in 2025 - Cahit Barkin Ozer - MediumURL: https://cbarkinozer.medium.com/software-development-with-ai-in-2025-238d3e8c0ac7SitemapOpen in app Sign in Medium LogoWriteSearch Sign in Software Development with AI in 2025 Cahit Barkin Ozer43 min read · Aug 26, 2025-- My summary notes from the “SWE Agents: AI Engineer World’s Fair 2025” presentations that is about software engineering agents / code agents. The AIEngineer channel on YouTube (link) hosts talks, workshops, events, and training for AI engineers. Recently, the channel organised an event featuring some of the most prominent names in the generative AI space. According to the channel, the goal of the event was to explore this vision: The final form of the AI Engineer is the autonomous, non-human SWE Agent who can both code alongside you in the IDE (Inner Loop) and convert PRDs to PRs (Outer Loop). How good are the SOTA SWE Agents today — good enough that companies will stop hiring AI Engineers, or good enough that engineers skilled at managing SWE Agents become the hottest job? Press enter or click to view image in full size I watched the playlist from the event and took notes on what I learned.Does AI Boost Developer Productivity? (100k Devs Study) — Yegor Denisov-Blanch, Stanford Press enter or click to view image in full size The speaker, a researcher at Stanford, challenges the hype that AI will soon replace software developers, a narrative fueled by statements from figures like Mark Zuckerberg. Based on a large-scale, three-year study involving over 100,000 engineers from 600+ companies, the speaker argues that while AI does increase productivity, its impact is highly nuanced and not a “one-size-fits-all” solution. Part 1: The Problem with Existing Research The speaker critiques current studies on AI’s impact on coding for three main reasons: Focus on Misleading Metrics: Many studies measure productivity by the number of commits or pull requests. However, the speaker’s data shows AI often increases “rework” — code written to fix the AI’s own recent mistakes — which inflates activity without adding real value. Unrealistic Test Scenarios: Studies often use “greenfield” tasks (building something from scratch) where AI excels at writing boilerplate code. This doesn’t reflect the reality for most developers who work on complex, existing “brownfield” codebases. Unreliable Surveys: Asking developers to self-report their productivity is as effective as “flipping a coin.” The study found almost no correlation between perceived and actual measured productivity. Part 2: A Better Methodology The Stanford research team developed a model that automates expert code review. Instead of counting lines or commits, it analyses the functional impact of code changes, distinguishing between: Added Functionality (Green): New features. Refactoring (Blue): Improving existing code. Rework (Orange): Wasteful changes to recently written code. This allows for a more accurate measure of true output. Part 3: Key Findings Overall Productivity Gain is Modest: While AI can initially generate code 30–40% faster, a significant portion of that gain is lost to fixing the AI’s errors. The net productivity gain averages around 15–20%. Effectiveness Varies Greatly by Context: The impact of AI is not uniform. The study breaks it down into a matrix: Low Complexity / Greenfield (New Project): Highest gains (30–40%). Low Complexity / Brownfield (Existing Project): Good gains (15–20%). High Complexity / Greenfield (New Project): Modest gains (10–15%). High Complexity / Brownfield (Existing Project): Lowest gains (0–10%). This is where most professional development work happens.Language Popularity Matters: AI is very effective with popular languages like Python and JavaScript. For obscure languages (e.g., Cobol, Haskell), AI is not only unhelpful but can decrease productivity by generating bad code that developers must spend time fixing.Codebase Size and Context Windows are Major Hurdles: AI’s effectiveness decreases sharply as a codebase grows in size and complexity. Large context windows are not a silver bullet. The speaker cites research showing that even the best models’ performance degrades significantly as the context length increases, making it impractical to simply “feed” an entire large codebase to an AI. As a conclusion, AI is increasing productivity and should be used, but the productivity increase depends on the language, project and complexity of the code. Surveys never tell the truth because the perceived productivity change is always wrong, according to the previous works that evaluate this. The definition of productivity is functionality delivered across time.Claude Code & the evolution of agentic coding — Boris Cherny, Anthropic Press enter or click to view image in full size The speaker, Boris, a creator of Claude Code at Anthropic, presents a talk about the current state and future of AI-powered coding tools. The core argument is that AI models for coding are advancing at an exponential rate, but the “product” or user experience (UX) for interacting with them is still in its early stages. Because the “right” way to build these tools is not yet clear, Claude Code’s philosophy is to be simple, powerful, and unopinionated. Part 1: The Exponential Pace of Change The speaker frames the current moment by tracing the rapid evolution of programming in two parallel tracks: Programming Abstractions: From physical switchboards and punch cards to higher-level languages like Cobol and C++, and finally to modern languages (Go, Rust, TypeScript), which have begun to converge in their abstractions. Programming UX: From typewriter-like machines for punch cards to the first text editor (“Ed”), to graphical IDEs (Smalltalk-80), AI-powered auto-complete (Copilot), and now to natural language agents (Devin), which allow users to generate code from plain English prompts. The key takeaway is that while programming languages have somewhat stabilised, the underlying AI models and the UX for using them are on an exponential curve. Part 2: The Claude Code Philosophy: Simple, Low-Level, and Unopinionated Because the future of coding UX is uncertain, Anthropic’s approach with Claude Code is to start with the fundamentals and get out of the way. Intentionally Simple: It avoids flashy UI and complex scaffolding, starting as a terminal-first tool. This allows it to be general and work anywhere (iTerm, VS Code terminal, SSH sessions). Low-Level Access: The goal is to give developers direct, raw access to the model’s capabilities so they can discover the most effective workflows for themselves. Integrations as Minimal Layers: While terminal-first, it has integrations that add value without being overly prescriptive: IDE Integration: Provides better visual diffs and uses editor diagnostics. GitHub Integration: Allows Claude to run on any repo, using your own compute and keeping your data private. SDK: For users who want to build completely custom tools and UIs. Part 3: Practical Tips for Using Claude Code Effectively The speaker shares several workflows that go beyond simple code generation: Codebase Q&A: Excellent for onboarding new engineers, allowing them to ask questions about the codebase instead of bugging senior developers. It can also be used for tasks like summarising your work for a weekly standup. Teach It Your Tools: Unlike traditional IDE plugins, Claude can use your existing command-line tools. You can simply have it read a tool’s help manual (claude — run-help) to teach it how to use it. Plan Before Acting: A key workflow is to have Claude explore the codebase and create a plan before writing any code. The new Plan Mode (Shift+Tab) formalises this, forcing Claude to wait for user approval before executing tasks. Test-Driven Development (TDD): AI makes TDD much more practical. The recommended workflow is to ask Claude to write the tests first (and explicitly tell them they are expected to fail), commit them, and then ask him to write the code that makes the tests pass. Iterate Against a Target: This generalises the TDD principle. Claude performs significantly better if it has a target to iterate against, whether it’s a unit test, an integration test, or even a screenshot from a UI simulator. Part 4: Providing Context is Key To get the most out of Claude, you need to give it context. The tool provides several mechanisms for this: Claude.md: A markdown file in the root of your repository (or subfolders) where you can provide high-level context, architectural overviews, or instructions. Slash Commands (/): Create reusable workflows by placing markdown files in a special /.claude/commands folder. Memorisation (#): Use the pound/hash symbol to tell Claude to memorise a piece of information and add it to the Claude.md file. This is presented as an early experiment in figuring out the UX for memory in coding agents.The emerging skillset of wielding coding agents — Beyang Liu, Sourcegraph / Amp Press enter or click to view image in full size Beyang Liue, the CTO and co-founder of Sourcegraph, delivered a talk on the emerging skill set required to effectively use AI coding agents. He argues that we are in a new “era of agents” that requires a fundamental shift in how we build and interact with AI tools, moving beyond the paradigms established by earlier autocomplete and chatbot models. Part 1: The Current State: A “Spicy” Debate Beyang opens by highlighting the ongoing, often heated debate in the developer community about the real-world usefulness of AI coding agents. He references contrasting opinions from elite developers: Sceptics like Jonathan Blow (creator of Braid), who feel the hype outweighs the substance. Pragmatists like Jesse Frazelle (early Docker contributor) argue that while agents may not help the top 0.01% of programmers, they are immensely helpful for everyone else. Enthusiasts like Eric S. Raymond and others find the tools incredibly useful even at a high skill level. This discourse proves that best practices are still forming, and a common mistake is using today’s powerful agents with the same mindset as the simpler tools of six months ago. Part 2: The Three Eras of AI Coding Tools The rapid evolution of AI models has dictated the design of coding tools, leading to three distinct eras: The GPT-3 Era (Text Completion): The dominant UX was autocomplete (like early GitHub Copilot), where the AI simply completed what you were typing. The ChatGPT Era (Chatbots + RAG): The dominant UX was a chatbot combined with Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG). Users would copy-paste code into a chat window to provide context. The Current Era (Agents): Frontier models are now capable of tool use, which requires a completely new application architecture and user interaction model. Part 3: “Spicy Takes”: A New Design Philosophy for Agents Beyang argues that designing for the agentic era requires breaking from the best practices of the chatbot era. He presents several “controversial” design principles behind Sourcegraph’s agent, AMP: Agents should edit files directly. Don’t micromanage them by asking for permission at every step. The human’s role is to steer at a high level (“on top of the loop”), not manage individual changes (“in the loop”). A “thick client” is no longer necessary. You don’t need a full fork of VS Code. If the agent can be trusted to perform tasks, the UI can be minimal — like a simple text box. Move beyond “choose your model.” In the agentic world, the model and the tools are deeply coupled. Swapping out the “brain” is not trivial and often breaks functionality. Move beyond fixed pricing. Agents use more tokens but save valuable human time. Fixed pricing creates a perverse incentive for providers to use cheaper, “dumber” models that waste the user’s time. Embrace the Unix philosophy. Composable, command-line tools are more powerful than monolithic, vertically integrated applications. Build new apps from scratch. To truly leverage agentic capabilities, you can’t just bolt them onto an architecture designed for chatbots. This is compared to the leap from an AOL-style portal to the simplicity and power of a Google search box. Part 4: Lessons from Power Users: How to Use Agents Effectively By observing the most effective users of AMP, Beyang’s team identified several emergent best practices: Write long, detailed prompts. Treat the agent like a junior developer, not a search engine. Give it the same detailed context and instructions you would give a human colleague. Guide it to feedback loops. Explicitly tell the agent how to run your specific tests or build scripts. A fast feedback loop (e.g., using Playwright + Storybook for frontend) is critical for success. Use agents to understand code, not ignore it. Power users leverage agents for more thorough onboarding and code reviews. Ask the agent to summarise a complex PR or draw a diagram of the architecture to lower the activation energy for difficult tasks. Use sub-agents for complex tasks. For long-running tasks, spinning off a sub-agent for a specific feature can preserve the main agent’s context window and prevent its performance from degrading. Run multiple agents in parallel. The most advanced users assign different agents to work on different parts of a project simultaneously, coming back later to review the results. Wielding a coding agent is a high-ceiling skill that requires intentional practice. It’s not about making developers lazy but about augmenting their abilities to be 10–100x more effective. The key is to move past the chatbot mindset and embrace a new collaborative paradigm where the human provides high-level direction and the agent handles the detailed execution.Vibes won’t cut it — Chris Kelly, Augment Code Press enter or click to view image in full size The speaker, Chris Kelly, delivers a pragmatic and experience-driven counter-narrative to the hype that AI will soon replace software engineers. He argues that this view is promoted by people who “haven’t touched a production system in a very, very long time,” and that the real work of a professional software engineer goes far beyond simply generating code. Part 1: Why the “AI Will Replace Us” Hype is Wrong The speaker identifies several key reasons why AI-generated code is not a simple replacement for human engineers: Production is Not a Greenfield: Hype often comes from large tech companies where the codebases are massive and most architectural decisions have already been made. Generating a new button in that context is simple because there’s no “wiggle room.” In the real world, software requires constant, nuanced decision-making. AI Code is Still Code: AI-generated code must run in complex production systems, which have emergent, unpredictable behaviours. When something breaks at 2 AM, a “vibe” won’t fix it; a human engineer who understands the system’s nuances is required. History Repeats Itself: This isn’t the first time a technology has been predicted to end a profession. The DevOps and cloud transformations didn’t eliminate sysadmins; they elevated their roles and increased their pay. As the speaker puts it, “tractors didn’t get rid of farms, they just got rid of farm hands and horses.” Part 2: The Real Job of a Software Engineer A central theme is that we misunderstand the job if we think it’s just about writing code. Code is an Artefact, Not the Job: Just as blueprints are an artefact of an architect’s work, code is an artefact of a software engineer’s work. The real job involves making thousands of decisions about architecture, trade-offs, and safety. The Best Code is No Code: Quoting Jeff Atwood, the speaker reminds the audience that every line of code is a liability that must be maintained and debugged. The goal isn’t to generate more code, but to solve problems with as little code as possible. The Core Task is “Changing Software Safely”: This has been the job for 20 years and remains the most critical function of a software engineer. Part 3: Making AI Work for Professional Engineers The speaker notes that professional developers have been surprisingly slow to adopt AI, likely due to a healthy scepticism about its ability to handle production complexity. To bridge this gap, he offers two sets of advice: A. How to Prepare Your Codebase FOR AI: Have documented standards and practices. Ensure reproducible developer environments. Make testing easy and fast. Establish clear boundaries and well-defined tasks. He concludes that these are simply good software engineering practices — AI needs the same stable foundation that a human engineer needs to be productive. B. New Skills for Engineers in the AI Era: Code Review is the Most Important Skill: As agents write more code, the ability to critically review it for correctness, safety, and nuance becomes paramount. Our current tools for code review are insufficient for this new reality. Distrust the AI’s Language: Remember that an LLM “talks like a human but is a machine.” When it says, “I skimmed the file,” it’s just pattern-matching human excuses; it doesn’t understand the concept. Accept “Different” Code: Let go of the need for the AI to write code exactly as you would. If it’s functionally correct and follows style guides, that is good enough. Embrace the “Define, Create, Refine” Loop: Instead of expecting perfect code on the first try, use a structured process: Define: Have the agent help you write a plan (e.g., in a markdown file). Create: Have the agent execute that plan. Refine: Use your expertise to make the final tweaks and corrections. In conclusion, AI will enable software engineers to earn more money with greater ease. AI mimics the way developers work, and — just like humans — it rarely transforms an abstract idea into a finished project in one go; instead, it builds and refines it commit by commit. In this new landscape, software engineers will focus on documenting their preferred standards and methods, creating simple and repeatable work environments, setting up easily accessible automated testing systems, defining products with clear boundaries, and making deliberate trade-offs. However, in novel or highly specialised domains, AI will be less capable and will require more hands-on use. While there’s significant hype around LLMs, their real capabilities are often left vague, and their outputs remain non-deterministic. This uncertainty is why developers approach them with more caution than other emerging technologies. Technical expertise will still be essential, as developers will continue to make and assess key decisions — but over time, the ability to review code effectively may become even more valuable than the ability to write it from scratch.Software Development Agents: What Works and What Doesn’t — Robert Brennan, AllHands/OpenHands Press enter or click to view image in full size Robert Brennan, creator of the open-source agent Open Hands (previously OpenDevin), presents a vision for the future of software development where “coding is going away, but software engineering is not.” He argues that as AI agents become more capable, the role of a developer will shift from the mechanical task of writing code to the more critical, high-level work of strategic thinking. Part 1: The Shifting Role of the Software Engineer The core thesis is that AI-driven development, when done correctly, will free up developers from the “inner loop” (write code, run code, repeat). This allows them to spend more time on what truly matters: Empathising with the user: What problems are we trying to solve? Business-level objectives: How does this work align with our organisation’s goals? Architectural planning: How can we build this in a way that sets us up for future success? AI agents excel at the repetitive, tactical work, but humans are essential for the strategic, empathetic, and long-term thinking that defines professional software engineering. Part 2: How Coding Agents Work (Under the Hood) To use agents effectively, it helps to understand their fundamental mechanics. The Agent Loop: An agent operates in a continuous loop: The LLM (the “brain”) decides on the next action to take. The agent executes that action in the real world using its tools. It gathers feedback (e.g., file contents, command output). The feedback is passed back to the LLM to inform the next decision. The Core Tools & Their Challenges: Code Editor: Uses efficient methods like diffs or find-and-replace to make tactical edits, rather than rewriting entire files. Terminal: Must handle complexities like long-running commands and parallel processes. Web Browser: The most complicated tool; it must parse messy HTML, interact with elements, and scroll through content. This is an area of active research. Sandboxing is Critical: To prevent accidents (like rm -rf on your home directory), agents run in isolated Docker containers. When giving them access to APIs (like GitHub or AWS), it is crucial to use tightly scoped credentials following the “principle of least privilege.” Part 3: Best Practices for Working with Agents The speaker provides several key pieces of advice for integrating agents into a workflow: Start Small: Begin with simple, well-defined tasks like fixing lint errors, resolving merge conflicts, or addressing specific PR feedback. These are “chores” that agents handle very well. As your intuition grows, you can give them larger tasks. Be Clear and Specific: Tell the agent not just what to do, but how to do it. Mention specific files, functions, or frameworks you want it to use. This makes it faster and more accurate. Code is Cheap: Embrace experimentation. It’s easy to have an agent spin up a prototype. If it doesn’t work, throw it away and start a new prompt with what you’ve learned. Don’t get attached to AI-generated code. REVIEW THE CODE (The Most Important Rule): This is the speaker’s most critical piece of advice. Blindly merging AI-generated code leads to massive tech debt and unmaintainable systems. You must review the code, run it yourself, and verify that it solves the problem correctly. A Human Must Be Accountable: Early versions of Open Hands showed PRs as being authored by the agent, which led to a lack of ownership. Now, the human who initiated the task is the author. A human must always be in the loop and responsible for the final code. Part 4: Practical Use Cases The speaker highlights several “Day 1” use cases where agents excel: Resolving Merge Conflicts: A rote, tedious task that agents can handle 99% of the time. Addressing PR Feedback: The agent can “do what that guy said” based on a reviewer’s comments. Fixing Bugs & Infrastructure Changes: Great for quick fixes or looking up esoteric syntax (e.g., in Terraform). Database Migrations & Test Coverage: Agents are good at following best practices that humans sometimes forget. Building Internal Apps: For non-production tools, you can be a bit more relaxed with reviews and quickly spin up useful applications. AI coding agents are powerful tools that are fundamentally changing the developer workflow. By offloading the mechanical aspects of coding, they empower engineers to focus on higher-value work, but this requires adopting new skills centred on clear communication, critical review, and ultimate human accountability.Devin 2.0 and the Future of SWE — Scott Wu, Cognition Press enter or click to view image in full size The speaker, Scott Wu, presents a framework for understanding the explosive growth in AI-driven software engineering, which he terms a “Moore’s Law for AI Agents.” He argues that the capability of AI to handle coding tasks is doubling every 70 days, leading to a 16x to 64x improvement in a single year. The core insight is that this staggering pace means the most important problems, the most effective interfaces, and the most critical capabilities are in a constant state of reinvention, changing entirely every few months as the technology ascends to a new tier of competence. Part 1: The “Moore’s Law” Framework for AI Agents The talk is built on the premise that the progress in AI for coding is not linear but exponential, which has profound implications for how we build and interact with these tools. Exponential Growth in Code: The speaker asserts that while general AI capabilities double roughly every seven months, the doubling time for software engineering tasks is a mere 70 days. This means that every year, an AI agent’s ability to perform complex, uninterrupted work grows by a factor of 16 to 64. This explains the rapid journey from simple tab-completion 18 months ago to today’s agents that can handle tasks taking hours of human time. Constantly Shifting Bottlenecks: A key consequence of this speed is that the “right” way to build or use an AI agent is a moving target. The primary bottleneck, the most important feature to add, and the ideal user interface all change with each doubling of capability. The solution to yesterday’s problem is often inadequate for today’s more powerful agent. Part 2: The Evolutionary Tiers of an AI Agent The speaker illustrates this evolution by walking through the distinct capability tiers Devin has achieved over the last year, with each tier representing a fundamentally different type of problem to solve. Summer 2023: Repetitive Migrations: The first reliable, high-value use case was handling large-scale, repetitive tasks like migrating from JavaScript to TypeScript or upgrading framework versions. The core challenge was instruction following. The solution was building “playbooks” for step-by-step execution and a “memory” system to incorporate human feedback and improve over time. Fall 2023: Isolated Bugs & Features: The next step was handling small, contained tasks akin to what an intern might be assigned (e.g., “reorder this dropdown”). The primary challenge became the environment. This required the ability to set up a repository, run linters and CI checks, and work within a clean, remote VM that could be snapshotted and rolled back. Late Fall 2023: Broader, Multi-File Bugs: As the agent grew more capable, it could tackle complex issues that required diagnosing problems across multiple files. The key challenge shifted to understanding the codebase. This meant seeing the code not just as text but as a structured hierarchy, using tools like language servers and git history to build context, which led to natural integrations with platforms like Slack for issue assignment. Spring 2024: Collaborative, Complex Tasks: The agent began tackling more ambiguous tasks where the human doesn’t know the solution upfront (e.g., “improve this architecture”). The challenge was to enable a human-AI collaborative workflow. This led to the in-IDE experience (Devin 2.0) and tools for code-aware search, allowing the human to explore the problem with the agent before setting it off to work autonomously. Present (June 2024): Autonomous Backlog Management: The current frontier is the ability to autonomously tackle a backlog of tasks from issue trackers like Jira. The central challenge is achieving a higher level of autonomy through confidence and self-verification. This requires the agent to not only assess its understanding of a task but, crucially, to perform its iterative testing to validate its work before delivering a PR. Part 3: The Shifting Nature of the Problem & The Future The speaker concludes by reflecting on how the very definition of the “AI for code” problem has changed and what lies ahead. From Text Prediction to a Full System: The problem has evolved far beyond simple text prediction. It now encompasses a full system of capabilities: working with human feedback and planning tools (Slack, Jira), understanding complex project context, running its shell commands to debug, and most importantly, testing its outputs. The Next 12 Months: Scott Wu confidently predicts another 16x to 64x improvement over the coming year. He anticipates the next major leap will be from tackling individual tasks to handling entire projects. In conclusion, the journey of building an AI software engineer is not a linear path of making one thing incrementally better. It is a series of discrete, revolutionary steps, where each 2x improvement in capability unlocks a new class of problems and demands a new set of solutions. The focus has shifted from text to tools, from instructions to collaboration, and now from execution to self-verification. The exponential trend suggests that the landscape of software engineering will continue to transform at a breathtaking pace.Beyond the Prototype: Using AI to Write High-Quality Code — Josh Albrecht, Imbue Press enter or click to view image in full size Josh Albrecht introduces Sculptor, an experimental AI coding agent from Imbue designed to bridge the “trust gap” between AI-generated code and production-ready software. Instead of just generating code, Sculptor’s primary goal is to help developers trust that code by using AI to proactively prevent and detect problems. The tool provides synchronous feedback as code is written, focusing on early defect identification through planning, documentation, automated testing, and intelligent analysis. Detailed SummaryThe Core Problem: The “Trust Gap” in AI Coding Current AI coding tools can generate a lot of code quickly, but this output often isn’t reliable enough for production environments. Developers are left with a difficult choice: manually review every line or blindly trust the AI, neither of which is ideal. Sculptor aims to solve this by providing a “third option”: using another AI system to analyse the code for quality, safety, and correctness.Sculptor’s Philosophy: Proactive Defect Management High-quality software is defined by a low number of defects. To improve quality, problems must be identified as early as possible in the development process. Sculptor provides immediate, synchronous feedback, rather than acting as a late-stage tool like a pull request reviewer.Key Strategies for Preventing ProblemsSculptor integrates several features to stop errors before they happen: Learn: The agent can research existing solutions and technologies to avoid redundant work. Plan: It encourages a “plan-first” workflow, where the AI must outline its approach before writing any code. Write Specs & Docs: Sculptor treats documentation as a core part of the workflow and can detect when code and documentation go out of sync, making them easier to maintain. Enforce a Strict Style Guide: The tool makes suggestions to guide the AI toward best practices (e.g., using immutable code to prevent race conditions).Key Strategies for Detecting ProblemsWhen mistakes are made, Sculptor uses multiple methods to find them: Run Linters: It automatically runs linters (like ruff, pylint) and uses the AI to fix the tedious errors they find. It’s smart enough to only flag new errors introduced by the agent, so it works in imperfect codebases. Write & Run Tests: The agent makes test generation easy. The presentation highlights several best practices: Write code in a functional style (no side effects) for easier testing. Use LLMs to generate hundreds of “unhappy path” tests to find bugs. Focus on high-level integration tests that verify user-facing behaviour. Measure test coverage to ensure the generated code is adequately tested. Ask an LLM: A separate LLM acts as a reviewer to check for logic flaws, style violations, security issues (like race conditions), and incomplete implementations.The Future and Call to Action Once problems are identified (“a problem well-stated is half-solved”), AI agents are very effective at fixing them, often by simply trying multiple solutions in a sandboxed environment. The future of development will involve an ecosystem of specialised AI tools working together. Imbue is looking to integrate other developer tools into Sculptor and invites interested developers to sign up for the research preview and consider joining their team.Windsurf everywhere, doing everything, all at once — Kevin Hou, Windsurf Press enter or click to view image in full size According to Kevin Hou, the “secret sauce” behind Windsurf’s success is the concept of a shared timeline between the human developer and the AI. This allows the AI to understand the full context of a software engineering task, making it feel like it’s “reading the user’s mind.” Software Engineering is More Than Code Kevin argues that traditional AI coding tools focus too narrowly on just writing code. A real developer’s workflow involves a wide range of activities and tools outside the code editor, including: Planning & Context: Slack, Google Docs, Jira, Figma Coding & Development: The IDE, terminal commands, and web browsers Deployment & Review: GitHub (pull requests), CI/CD, deployment services (like Netlify) Windsurf’s Strategy: Be Everywhere, Do Everything To address this complex reality, Windsurf’s strategy is twofold: Be Everywhere (Read): Ingest context from every source a developer uses, from code files to Slack messages, design documents, and project management tickets. Do Everything (Write/Act): Empower the AI to take action across all these surfaces, not just editing code. This includes running terminal commands, visually iterating in a browser, creating pull requests, automatically reviewing code, and deploying to the web in one click. Introducing “SWE-1”: A Model for WorkflowsRecognising that standard benchmarks (like SWE-bench) don’t capture the messiness of real-world development, Windsurf built its software engineering model: SWE-1. SWE-1 is trained specifically on software engineering workflows, not just code generation. SWE-1 excels at assisting users mid-task and handling complex, multi-step processes. SWE-1 achieves near-frontier model performance at a fraction of the cost and with a much smaller team. The Vision and Flywheel Windsurf’s goal is a future where the AI handles 99% of the work, with the human only providing final approval. This is achieved through a “data flywheel”: Ship: Release the best product with integrated tools Learn: Users provide real-world feedback on what works and what’s missing. Build: Use these insights to train a better model (SWE-1) and improve the agent’s capabilities. Repeat.Useful General Intelligence — Danielle Perszyk, Amazon AGI Press enter or click to view image in full size Danielle Perszyk proposes a different vision for the future of AI agents, focusing on co-evolving with them to augment human intelligence rather than simply creating autonomous “thinking machines.”The “Controlled Hallucination” Analogy:Danielle begins by comparing AI to the human brain, which she describes as a “prediction machine” that experiences “controlled hallucinations.” Our brains don’t see reality directly; they predict it and use sensory input to correct errors. This ability to hallucinate is a feature, not a bug, as it allows for flexible, abstract thought. Current AI also hallucinates but lacks the “control,” making it unreliable for general-purpose tasks.Two Competing Visions of AI:The talk contrasts two historical goals for AI: The Standard Vision (Thinking Machines): To build AI that is as smart as or smarter than humans, giving the AI more agency. The Augmentation Vision (Thinking Humans): Inspired by Douglas Engelbart (inventor of the computer mouse), this vision aims to build AI that makes humans smarter and gives us more agency. The speaker’s lab champions the second vision, arguing that simply making AI smarter could lead to problems like reduced human agency (e.g., mindless scrolling, echo chambers).Introducing NovaAct:NovaAct is Amazon’s research preview of an agent built on this augmentation philosophy. Function: It’s designed to interact with visual user interfaces (UIs) in a browser, just like a human. This is crucial because most of the web lacks APIs for machines to use. How it Works: Developers can use a simple SDK with an “act call” to translate natural language commands into on-screen actions (clicking, typing, scrolling). Focus: The initial focus is on making these small, “atomic” actions extremely reliable, providing a solid foundation for building complex workflows.The True Nature of General Intelligence:The speaker argues that the concept of a standalone AGI is a category error. True general intelligence, as seen in humans, is not an individual quality but a social and co-evolutionary one. Human Evolution: Our intelligence didn’t just come from bigger brains; it emerged from our ability to connect our minds through language. The key evolutionary adaptation was developing “models of mind”, the ability to infer what others are thinking. Implication for AI: General intelligence cannot exist in a vacuum or a single model. It must emerge through interaction within a shared environment.The Path Forward: Co-evolving with Agents:To make agents truly reliable and aligned, they must eventually develop their own “models of our minds” to understand our higher-level goals. This can be achieved through a feedback loop (a “flywheel”): Build Useful Products: Create agents that solve real-world problems. Gather Interaction Data: As people use these products, they generate data on human-agent interaction. Advance the Models: This data is used to train agents to understand human intent better. Augment Humans: Improved agents become more useful tools, enhancing our productivity and creativity. The ultimate goal is for agents to become a “collective subconscious,” handling repetitive tasks and helping humans think more strategically, thus creating a future where AI and humans collectively become smarter together.Real World Development with GitHub Copilot and VS Code — Harald Kirschner, Christopher Harrison Press enter or click to view image in full size Press enter or click to view image in full size Harald Kirschner breaks down “Vibe Coding” into three distinct stages of maturity: YOLO Vibes: This is the initial, chaotic stage focused on creativity and speed. It’s characterised by giving the AI high-level prompts, auto-accepting changes, and not reviewing the code. This stage is excellent for rapid prototyping, learning new technologies, and personal projects. Structured Vibes: This stage aims for balance and sustainability by adding guardrails to the process. The goal is to produce maintainable, consistent, and higher-quality code. The Structured Toolbox for this stage includes: Workspace Instructions: High-level overviews of the codebase are included in all chat requests. Dynamic Instructions: Detailed, domain-specific expertise that is auto-included based on the files being worked on. Custom Context Tools: MCP servers for integrating with Figma, Playwright, developer docs, and deep research. Problems & Task Tools: Using the agent to auto-fix problems and compile errors after changes. Auto-generated Commits: Creating atomic commits for each working stage. Fine-grained Review: The ability to pause/stop the agent for review, edit tool inputs, and trust specific read-only tools.Spectrum Vibes (Spec-Driven): This is the most advanced stage, focused on achieving scale, reliability, and velocity through a specification-driven workflow. Tips for this stage include drafting specs from artefacts (like meeting transcripts), front-loading review steps (like writing tests before code), and keeping the spec in sync with AI assistance. The Spec-Driven VS Code Toolbox features: Reusable Prompts: Personal or team-shared prompts that can limit tools, specify models, and attach relevant files. Run Tests & Debug: Applying Test-Driven Development (TDD) and letting the agent fix broken tests and improve coverage. Custom DevOps Tools/Context: Using MCP tools for CI/CD, database schemas, and project tracking. Task-specific Models: Using powerful models for complex reasoning and planning, and faster models for implementation. Common Mistakes and Solutions The presentation highlights common pitfalls and how to address them:Problem: “It got confused after working for minutes.” Solution: Work in stages, broken down in a spec doc, and commit frequently.Problem: “The code it writes doesn’t pass tests.” Solution: Prompt the agent to run tests and builds before and after edits.Problem: “Copilot uses the wrong library/syntax/etc.” Solution: Add custom instructions outlining the project, architecture, and best practices.Problem: “It uses the wrong tools.” Solution: Disable unused tools, prompt for required ones, and use the pause/cancel feature to make corrections. Main Takeaways Experiment: Developers should experiment to understand the AI’s capabilities and find a workflow that suits them. Commit Often: Use version control as a safety net. After a successful AI generation, commit the changes to create a restore point. Iterate and Refine: Continuously improve your prompts, instructions, and custom tools as you identify weaknesses or recurring mistakes in the AI’s output. Structure is Key for Scale: While “YOLO Vibes” are fun for exploration, the “Structured” and “Spectrum” approaches are what make AI-driven development practical and reliable for professional teams.Your Coding Agent Just Got Cloned And Your Brain Isn’t Ready — Rustin Banks, Google Jules Press enter or click to view image in full size Rustin, a Product Manager at Google Labs, introduces Jules, an asynchronous AI coding agent designed to handle background tasks in parallel, freeing up developers to focus on more creative work. He contrasts the traditional “serial” developer workflow (one task at a time) with a new “parallel” approach enabled by AI agents. Jules, as a remote cloud-based agent, can tackle multiple tasks simultaneously. Rustin identifies two main types of parallelism that have emerged: Multitasking: The expected use case, where an agent works on several different tasks from a backlog at the same time (e.g., adding a feature, running a security audit, and updating a dependency). Multiple Variations: An emergent behaviour where a developer gives a single complex task to multiple agents, asking each to try a different approach (e.g., “Implement this feature using Library A,” “Try it with Library B,” and “Use a test-first approach”). The developer can then review, test, and choose the best outcome. Demo:Rustin demonstrates Jules on a conference schedule website. He tasks Jules with several jobs in parallel: Add a testing framework (trying both Jest and Playwright simultaneously to compare coverage). Add an “Add to Google Calendar” button. Implement AI-powered summaries for session descriptions. Run an accessibility audit and improve Lighthouse scores. He shows the successful implementation of the calendar feature, noting that all tasks were completed in about an hour in the background, managed by Jules. Key Takeaways & Best Practices: The secret to parallel work is a clear definition of success. To avoid being overwhelmed by reviewing pull requests, you must provide the agent with a clear, verifiable goal (e.g., “Don’t stop until all these tests pass”). Use a robust merge and test framework to bring all the parallel work together efficiently. A good prompt for an agent includes: A brief overview, the success criteria, helpful context (like documentation), and a simple approach. Embrace an “abundance mindset” by using parallelism to try multiple solutions and explore ideas you wouldn’t normally have time for.Ship Production Software in Minutes, Not Months — Eno Reyes, Factory Press enter or click to view image in full size Eno from Factory argues that we are on the verge of a fundamental shift in software development, moving from a human-driven era to an agent-driven one. He calls this new paradigm “agent-native development.” He critiques the current approach of simply “sprinkling AI on top” of existing tools, comparing it to Henry Ford’s famous quote: “If I had asked people what they wanted, they would have said faster horses.” Instead of incremental improvements, Factory envisions a future where developers delegate the majority of their tasks to AI agents. Here are the key takeaways from his talk:The Central Importance of Context:The most critical factor for successful AI agents is not the intelligence of the LLM, but the quality and completeness of the context they receive. Failures often happen because the AI is missing crucial information from meetings, design documents, or Slack conversations. The solution is to create platforms that centralise context from all engineering tools and data sources.The New Role of the Software Engineer:AI agents will not replace software engineers; they will amplify their capabilities. The developer’s role will shift from executing tasks (the “inner loop” of writing code) to orchestrating systems of agents (the “outer loop”). The most valuable skills will be clear thinking and the ability to communicate effectively with both humans and AI.Agent-Native Development in Practice:Eno provides several examples of how their agents, called “droids,” operate across the entire software lifecycle: Coding: A droid can take a task, search the codebase, check git history, review past interactions (“memory”), create a detailed plan, ask the user for clarification, and then execute the plan to write code, run tests, and generate a pull request that passes CI. Planning & Design: This is where agents can have the most impact. By feeding a droid user feedback, architecture documents, and meeting transcripts, it can identify patterns, highlight constraints, and help generate a comprehensive Product Requirements Document (PRD). This PRD can then be automatically converted into a roadmap of tasks for other coding droids to work on in parallel. Site Reliability Engineering (SRE): When an incident occurs, a droid can instantly pull context from logs, metrics, past incidents, and team discussions to provide a full root cause analysis (RCA) and a mitigation plan in minutes, shifting teams from reactive to predictive operations.Documentation is No Longer a Burden:In an agent-native world, process and documentation (PRDs, design docs, meeting transcripts) are not just for humans. They become a knowledge base and a map for AI agents to learn and imitate how the team thinks and works, making them an essential asset. Press enter or click to view image in full size Eno concludes by emphasising that the future belongs to developers who learn to work with agents, not those who wait for AI to do the work for them. He introduces his company, Factory, as an enterprise platform built to facilitate this transition, focusing on critical issues like security, auditability, and liability for agent-based systems.Vibe Coding with Confidence — Itamar Friedman, Qodo Press enter or click to view image in full size The next significant leap in AI-powered software development will move beyond simple code generation (“vibe coding”) to “vibe coding with confidence.” Itay Friedman argues that this evolution requires a shift from IDE-centric tools to holistic, workflow-based systems, and surprisingly, the Command Line Interface (CLI) is poised to be the leading interface for this new era. Key Points:The Evolution of AI for Developers (Gen 1.0 to 3.0): Gen 1.0 (Autocomplete): Tools like GitHub Copilot that complete lines of code. Liked by developers, but it is not a game-changer. Gen 2.0 (Dialogic Chat): Tools like ChatGPT that can generate large blocks of code from prompts. Loved by many, but for enterprise use, it often creates more review work and doesn’t guarantee high quality. Gen 3.0 (The Game-Changer): The future is moving from chat to commands. Developers will command AI agents like team members to execute end-to-end workflows across the entire Software Development Lifecycle (SDLC), not just within the code editor.“Vibe Coding” vs. “Vibe Coding with Confidence”: “Vibe coding,” a term popularised by Andrej Karpathy, describes the process of rapidly generating code based on a prompt or idea. This approach is effective for new, simple, or “greenfield” projects. However, it falls short in large, existing enterprise codebases where maintainability, testing, and quality are critical. Friedman cites Karpathy himself, stating that for code he “professionally cares about,” a more rigorous process is needed involving context and structured workflows. Confidence comes from integrating AI into all parts of development — planning, writing, testing, and reviewing in a holistic, automated way.Why the CLI is the Future Interface: Cross-SDLC Operation: Unlike IDE plugins, a CLI is not confined to the editor. It can orchestrate tasks across the entire development lifecycle (e.g., from ticket creation to deployment). Automation and Workflows: The CLI is inherently scriptable. Agents and tasks can be chained (“piped”) together to create powerful, repeatable workflows (e.g., generate code -> run coverage agent -> run review agent). Agent-to-Agent (A2A) Communication: The CLI provides a natural environment for multiple specialised agents to run in the background, communicate, and collaborate on complex tasks. Flexibility: A CLI tool can generate purpose-built user interfaces on the fly (e.g., a dedicated interface for a code review) rather than being locked into a monolithic IDE.Codto’s Multi-Agent Solution and CLI Tool: Friedman introduces Codto, which is building a holistic, multi-agent platform to enable this next generation of development. During the talk, he announces the soft launch of Codto’s new CLI tool. He demonstrates how the CLI can be used to create a new, specialised AI agent (a “review agent”) with a simple command and then use that agent to perform tasks. This tool allows developers to orchestrate various agents (for code research, review, test coverage, etc.) to build trusted, high-quality software with confidence. The future of software development will involve “swarms of agents,” each specialising in different tasks. The CLI will be the crucial orchestration layer that allows developers to command these agents, build robust workflows, and ultimately code with both speed and confidence.Ship Agents that Ship: A Hands-On Workshop — Kyle Penfound, Jeremy Adams, Dagger Press enter or click to view image in full size This video is a workshop by Kyle and Jeremy from Dagger, demonstrating how to build a software development AI agent using the Dagger platform. The core idea is to create reliable, production-ready CI/CD functions (like building and testing an application) and then give those exact functions to an AI agent as its tools. The workshop culminates in deploying this agent to GitHub, where it can automatically pick up feature requests from GitHub issues and submit pull requests with the completed code. What is Dagger? Dagger is presented as a workflow engine that allows you to define your software engineering workflows (like CI/CD pipelines and development environments) as code. Key Idea: While Docker containerises an application, Dagger containerises an entire workflow. This makes the workflow portable, ensuring it runs the same on a local machine as it does in any CI environment (like GitHub Actions). Building Blocks: You write functions in languages like Python, Go, or TypeScript that chain together core primitives like containers, files, directories, and now, LLMs. Cross-Language Interoperability: Dagger modules written in different languages can be used together seamlessly. A Python project can install and use a Dagger module written in Go, for instance. Workshop Walkthrough The workshop is broken down into three main parts:Building the CI Pipeline (The Tools) The first step is to create a standard CI pipeline for a sample web application. Using Dagger, they write functions in Python to build the application, run tests, and publish it. These functions define the exact container environments and commands needed for each task. This creates a set of reliable, battle-tested tools that any developer or CI system can use.Creating the AI Agent (The “Brain”) Next, they add an AI agent that can use the tools created in the first step. They create a dedicated workspace module for the agent, giving it a limited and specific set of tools: read_file() and write_file() to edit code. list_files() to understand the project structure. Crucially, a test() function that calls the same test function from the main CI pipeline. They write a development function which acts as the agent. This function takes a natural language “assignment” (e.g., “make the main page say hello workshop people”). A prompt is crafted to instruct the LLM on how to behave: Analyse the project, make changes, and most importantly, run the tests to validate its work before finishing. Key Feature: The entire process runs in a sandboxed container. The agent doesn’t modify the local file system directly; it works on a Dagger directory object and returns a new, modified directory upon completion.Deploying the Agent to GitHub The final goal is to automate this agent within a typical developer workflow. They create a new function called develop_issue that wraps the agent. This function: Uses a pre-built Dagger module from the “Daggerverse” to read the content of a GitHub issue. Passes that content as the assignment to the development agent. Takes the completed code from the agent. Automatically creates a new branch and opens a pull request with the agent’s changes. This entire workflow is triggered by a simple GitHub Actions file. When a GitHub issue is given the label “develop,” the action runs a single command: dagger call develop_issue…, kicking off the entire process. Key Takeaways and Concepts Agents with Real Developer Tools: The main principle is to avoid letting agents guess commands. Instead, you provide them with the same high-level, reliable functions (build, test) that your human developers and CI systems already use. Sandboxed and Reproducible: All agent operations are isolated in containers, ensuring safety and consistency. The agent’s work is immutable, making the process predictable. Abstraction and Portability: The complex agent logic is abstracted into a single Dagger function that can be called with a simple CLI command, making it easy to integrate into any system (like GitHub Actions, Jenkins, etc.). Debugging and Visualisation: They briefly show Dagger Cloud, a tool that provides a visual trace of the agent’s entire thought process, including every tool it considers, every function it calls, and the outputs it receives. This is crucial for debugging and refining agent behaviour.AI-powered entomology: Lessons from millions of AI code reviews — Tomas Reimers, Graphite The speaker, Tomas, discusses his company’s product, Diamond, an AI-powered code reviewer. The tool was created to address a growing problem: While AI is writing more code, it’s also introducing more bugs. Their initial idea was to use AI to find the bugs that AI creates. Initial Findings & The Core Problem:Early experiments using LLMs to review code were promising, successfully catching real bugs. However, they also produced many frustrating and incorrect suggestions (hallucinations), such as recommending code be reverted for no reason or misunderstanding how CSS works. This led to a loss of developer confidence. The Key Insight: The 2x2 FrameworkThe team realised that the problem wasn’t just whether an AI can find a bug, but also whether a human developer wants that type of feedback from an AI. They developed a 2x2 matrix to categorise code review comments: LLM Capability: Can an LLM reliably catch this? (Yes/No) Human Receptiveness: Do humans want to receive this feedback from an LLM? (Yes/No) This created four quadrants: Press enter or click to view image in full size Top-Right (The Goal): Things LLMs can catch AND humans want to receive (e.g., logical bugs, performance/security issues, accidentally committed code). Top-Left (Annoying): Things LLMs can catch but humans DON’T want (e.g., pedantic, low-value comments like “add a comment,” “extract this logic into a function,” “add tests”). Bottom-Right (Not Yet Possible): Things humans want but LLMs CAN’T catch (e.g., “tribal knowledge” about why code is a certain way, which isn’t documented anywhere). How do we be sure that we are in the right top corner? Press enter or click to view image in full size Diamond’s code review comments are mostly about bugs, style and documentation. To fine-tune their AI, Graphite focused on two key metrics to ensure it stayed in the “goal” quadrant: Accuracy (Can it catch it?): They implemented an upvote/downvote system on AI comments. A low downvote rate (currently <4%) indicates the AI is not hallucinating. Actionability (Do humans want it?): They measured the percentage of comments that led to an actual code change within the same pull request. They found that, surprisingly, only about 50% of human-generated comments lead to a change. By carefully prompting their AI to focus only on the types of feedback in the “goal” quadrant, Graphite’s Diamond tool achieved an actionability rate of 52%, slightly surpassing the human baseline. The talk concludes that AI-powered code review can be highly effective, but only when it is constrained to provide feedback that is both within the AI’s capabilities and genuinely valuable and welcome to developers.To the moon! Navigating deep context in legacy code with Augment Agent — Forrest Brazeal, Matt Ball Press enter or click to view image in full size This presentation, led by Forest and Matt from Augment Code, demonstrates how their AI tool, Augment Agent, can be used to understand, navigate, and modernise complex legacy codebases. The Core Idea: The primary challenge with legacy code is the steep learning curve required to understand its functionality before any changes can be made. Augment Agent is designed to accelerate this process significantly. Key Features of Augment Agent: Context Engine: A proprietary system that intelligently analyses a codebase to provide high-quality, relevant context to the AI models, resulting in more accurate outputs. IDE Integration: It works as a plugin within existing IDEs like VS Code and IntelliJ, so developers don’t have to switch their workflow. Enterprise-Grade Security: Built with security in mind, offering features like customer-managed encryption keys and compliance with new AI standards (ISO 420001). Two Modes: Chat Mode: For simple, interactive question-and-answer sessions about the code. Agent Mode: For performing complex, multi-step tasks autonomously. It can run in “auto mode” without needing constant user approval for each step. The Demonstration: The Apollo 11 Guidance Computer To showcase the agent’s capabilities, the presenters use the source code for the Apollo 11 Guidance Computer (AGC) as the ultimate legacy codebase. It’s a monolithic file written in assembly language from the 1960s, making it extremely difficult for a modern developer to read. The demo is split into two parts: Part 1: Understanding the Code (Using Chat Mode) The Problem: During the actual lunar landing, the astronauts received a “1202 program alarm” and had to ask Mission Control if it was safe to proceed. The Demo: Forest asks Augment Chat, “What does the 1202 program alarm do?” The Result: In seconds, the chat analyses the assembly code, identifies the octal value, and explains that the alarm means the computer’s memory is overloaded (“executive overflow”). It even pulls context from the web, explaining that the real-life cause was an external radar system being left on and that the computer correctly offloaded low-priority tasks. This shows how quickly the AI can provide deep, contextual understanding that would otherwise take hours of manual research. Part 2: Modernising and Executing Code (Using Agent Mode) The Problem: The presenters challenge the agent to implement the P65 automatic landing algorithm (which was in the original assembly code) in Python and then run a simulator to land the lunar module. A key Python file (descent.py) is intentionally missing. The Demo: Forest gives a simple prompt: “run simulator.py until it succeeds.” The Result: The agent autonomously executes a plan: It tries to run the simulator and sees that it fails because descent.py is missing. It analyses the rest of the codebase (including test files) to understand what the missing file should contain. It reads the original assembly code for the P65 algorithm. It writes the entire descent.py file, translating the assembly logic into readable Python. It successfully runs the simulator, showing the lunar module “landing” within the expected parameters. Practical Takeaways for Developers This demonstration isn’t just a “parlour trick.” The presenters outline a three-step strategy for using an AI agent on any real-world legacy project (e.g., migrating a Java 8 project to Java 17): Understand: Use the agent’s chat to ask questions and quickly get up to speed on what the codebase does, its conventions, and the purpose of specific files. Test: Use a combination of chat (to predict how code should behave) and agent mode (to write unit tests) to verify the existing functionality before making changes. Modernise: Instruct the agent to convert small, modular portions of the code into the desired modern language or framework, using the tests you created to ensure the new code works correctly.Mentoring the Machine — Eric Hou, Augment Code Press enter or click to view image in full size This talk by Eric, a member of the technical staff at Augment Code, is a personal story about how his company uses AI to build production-grade software. The central theme is that to use AI effectively, engineers must shift their role from implementers to mentors, treating AI agents as “perpetually junior engineers.” The Problem: The Chaotic Life of an Engineer Eric begins by describing a “terrible Tuesday,” a scenario familiar to most engineers: Being behind on a critical task. Getting derailed by a staging emergency (an on-call issue). Being interrupted by a new hire who needs mentoring.He highlights that this constant context-switching and firefighting is incredibly costly, leading to long hours with little sense of accomplishment. This chaos, he argues, has been normalized in the software industry. The Solution: A Shift from Firefighting to Orchestration Eric then replays the same “terrible Tuesday” but this time using Augment’s AI agents. The outcome is drastically different: The Critical Task: Instead of coding the component himself, he provides the AI agent with context, constraints, and desired outcomes. The agent builds a draft (an RFC) while he gets coffee. The Staging Emergency: He parallelizes the work. He assigns the component task to one agent, uses other agents to parse logs and find the faulty commit, and has a Slackbot manage communications. His role shifts from a firefighter to an orchestrator of AI workstreams. Mentoring the New Hire: He directs the new hire to an AI-powered Slackbot that has access to the company’s entire knowledge base (code, docs, etc.), allowing the new hire to get help without interrupting Eric. By the end of the day, all three problems are solved. The agents even completed a massive remediation task (upgrading a library across 12 services) that would normally take weeks of engineering time. The key takeaway is that his role transformed from implementation to evaluation. The Core Analogy: AI as a Junior Engineer The fundamental realization at Augment is that AI should be treated like a junior engineer. Similarities: Like a new hire, AI lacks business context, knowledge of your specific systems, and experience. It needs a structured environment to perform well. This is the “context or knowledge gap.” Differences: A junior engineer learns slowly but retains knowledge. An AI agent processes information and executes tasks incredibly fast but forgets everything between conversations. Conclusion: This makes AI a “perpetually junior engineer.” Therefore, human engineers must become “perpetual tech leads,” mentoring and guiding the AI rather than just assigning it tickets. The Organizational Challenge and a 3-Step Solution While individual engineers can see massive productivity gains, teams often struggle to scale this success. The blocker is the same knowledge gap that makes it hard to onboard new human employees. To solve this, organizations need to build a “knowledge infrastructure.” Eric proposes a three-step plan: Knowledge Gathering: Systematically map out and integrate all existing knowledge sources (docs, GitHub, Notion) and use tools to capture “lost” knowledge from meetings. Gain Familiarity: Introduce AI tools across the organization to let teams build the “muscle” of working with AI and, in turn, teach the AI about the company’s unique patterns and business logic. Leaning In: As trust builds, entrust AI with more complex tasks. Share successful AI configurations (called “memories” at Augment) across teams to create compound learning and scale individual successes. The Future: Parallel Exploration When an organization solves its knowledge infrastructure problem, it unlocks a new way of building software. Instead of a slow, linear process (design -> build -> test), teams can use AI to engage in parallel exploration. This means they can rapidly prototype, test, and validate multiple approaches at once, using real data to converge on the best solution. This, Eric concludes, can make software development more of a science and less of an art.The Agent Awakens: Collaborative Development with Copilot — Christopher Harrison, GitHub Press enter or click to view image in full size This video is a recording of a hands-on lab session led by Christopher Harrison and John Peek, Enterprise Advocates at GitHub. The session is designed to get developers started with GitHub Copilot, with a special focus on its newest and most powerful feature: the Coding Agent. The Core Concept: Context is Key The central theme of the presentation is that to use GitHub Copilot effectively, you must treat it like its tagline: “Your AI pair programmer.” The Brunch Analogy: The speaker explains that just as you’d provide more context to a human partner in a conversation (e.g., “I want brunch,” then “somewhere new,” then “I’m craving waffles”), you should provide iterative and specific context to Copilot. Context Beyond the Prompt: Effective context isn’t just about writing a good prompt. It also includes:Readable Code: Using clear function and variable names.Comments: Describing the intent of your code.Project Structure: A well-organized project is easier for Copilot to navigate. The main takeaway is that good, fundamental coding practices directly improve Copilot’s performance. Overview of Copilot’s Features The speaker breaks down the different ways to interact with Copilot: Code Completions: The original feature that suggests code as you type. Chat: Ask questions and get responses, useful for generating single files or getting explanations. Edits: The ability to make changes across multiple files in a project. Agent Mode (in the IDE): A more advanced mode where Copilot can navigate your project, run tasks like unit tests, and even self-correct based on errors. Coding Agent (on GitHub.com): The focus of the lab. You assign a GitHub issue to the agent, and it works in the background to analyze the issue, write the necessary code, and create a pull request for human review. Deep Dive into the Coding Agent The hands-on lab guides users through assigning a detailed GitHub issue to the Coding Agent. Key aspects of how it works were highlighted, especially its security features: Isolated Environment: It runs in a temporary, firewalled GitHub Actions environment with no internet access by default. Limited Permissions: It has no write access to the repository except for the new branch it creates for the pull request. Human Gating: It creates a draft pull request that requires a human to approve it. All CI/CD checks (like security scans and tests) are paused until a human clicks “Approve workflow runs.” Enforces Code Review: The person who assigned the issue to the agent is not allowed to review and merge their own PR, enforcing a two-person review process. Advanced Techniques and Q&A Highlights The second half of the session is a rich Q&A that covers advanced usage: Iteration: To provide feedback or request changes, simply add a comment to the original GitHub issue. The Coding Agent will see it and start a new session to incorporate the feedback. Providing Deeper Context: If Copilot gets something wrong, it’s usually a context issue. You can provide better context using: copilot.instructions file: A repository-level file that gives Copilot a high-level overview of the project, coding standards, and architecture. .instructions files: Directory or file-pattern-specific instructions to enforce consistent patterns (e.g., for creating all new React components). MCP (Model Context Protocol) Servers: Allows Copilot to connect to external data sources (like internal documentation or large codebases) to gain knowledge beyond what’s in the repo. Hierarchy of Context: There is no strict hierarchy. Copilot considers all available context (the issue, .instructions files, etc.), so it’s important to provide clear and consistent information. Source: TDD and AI: Quality in the DORA report | Google Cloud URL: https://cloud.google.com/discover/how-test-driven-development-amplifies-ai-success TDD and AI: Quality in the DORA report | Google Cloud cloud.google.com uses cookies from Google to deliver and enhance the quality of its services and to analyze traffic. Learn more OK, got it Page Contents How test-driven development amplifies AI success What is test-driven development? The TDD workflow: Red, green, refactor TDD and the age of AI The value of TDD principles for AI-assisted development Generate a solution Related solutions and products Further reading Take the next step Topics Test-driven development How test-driven development amplifies AI success AI's new reality: how TDD principles amplify your success. The 2025 DORA report finds that AI, while powerful, acts as an amplifier, making existing good practices even more effective. This means that foundational principles like those found in test-driven development (TDD) are more critical than ever. TDD can help you leverage AI to accelerate development, improve code quality, and increase team performance. To learn more about these findings, you can download the full DORA 2025 report. Download DORA report Get started for free 2025 DORA State of AI-assisted Software Development Report Download the report What is test-driven development? Test-driven development, or TDD, is a software development approach focused on writing tests before writing the actual code. It's a method where tests guide the entire development process, helping to create cleaner, more reliable, and better-designed code. It is a design practice that helps you think about what your code needs to do before you write it. For projects where writing code is not the main task, you may want to explore low-code and no-code solutions that can help you implement similar testing principles. The TDD workflow: Red, green, refactor The TDD workflow is a cycle that developers repeat over and over again. It is often called the "red, green, refactor" cycle, reflecting the status of your tests. Expand all Red: Write a failing test The first step is to write a test for a new piece of functionality. When you run this test, it should fail, which shows that the test is working correctly, and that the new feature isn't in the code yet. Green: Write the minimum code to pass the test With a failing test in place, the next step is to write the minimal amount of code required for it to pass. The sole objective is to pass the test, without concern for code perfection or elegance. Refactor: Clean up the code Once the test is passing, you can improve the code. Refactoring is the process of cleaning up your code's design without changing how it works. Since you have a passing test, you can make changes with confidence, knowing you won't break anything. TDD and the age of AI The DORA report highlights a complex relationship between AI and software delivery quality. While AI can boost individual productivity and perceived code quality, it can also increase instability if not paired with strong foundational practices. AI for testing: The report reveals that AI is being used for a variety of quality-related tasks, such as generating test cases and debugging. In fact, 62% of developers who write tests use AI to assist them. AI can also continuously analyze code for bugs and security vulnerabilities and dynamically generate new tests. Impact on code quality: AI adoption is associated with an increase in perceived code quality, with 59% of respondents reporting a positive impact. However, the report also notes that AI adoption is still associated with an increase in software delivery instability, which suggests a need for robust control systems, such as strong automated testing, to prevent new issues from being introduced. The report suggests that AI is seen as a productivity accelerator whose output should be funneled into pipelines that are guarded by version control, automated tests, and human review. A "trust but verify" approach: While AI can be helpful, the report notes that a significant portion of developers (30%) still report little to no trust in AI-generated code. This highlights the ongoing importance of human oversight and verification. The value of TDD principles for AI-assisted development The DORA report's findings show that the core principles of TDD are more relevant than ever. By focusing on quality and iterative improvement, teams can effectively harness the power of AI while mitigating its risks. The report emphasizes that AI's benefits are amplified when combined with these practices. Working in small batches The report confirms that working in small batches is a critical capability for high-performing teams. This practice allows teams to get feedback on changes quickly and more easily address issues. When AI is used in conjunction with this practice, it has a positive impact on product performance and helps to reduce friction within the development process. Confidence in changes Since TDD prioritizes writing tests first, it gives developers a high degree of confidence when making changes because the tests act as a safety net. This is particularly important in an AI-assisted environment where the volume and velocity of changes are dramatically increasing. The DORA report finds that using a version control system's rollback feature more frequently is associated with better team performance. Systemic approach The report highlights that successful AI adoption is a systems problem, not a tools problem. A well-designed internal platform provides the necessary guardrails and shared capabilities, ensuring that AI-generated code is safe and secure. These platforms help scale AI benefits from local optimizations to systemic organizational improvements.
